create a new section, as appropriate.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2020</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>By the late 19th and early 20th centuries, medical advancements such as <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anesthesia</a> and sterile techniques that could make surgery less risky, and availability of more advanced diagnostic devices such as <a href="/wiki/X-ray#Discovery" title="X-ray">X-rays</a>, continued to make hospitals a more attractive option for treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYThospital_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYThospital-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern hospitals measure various efficiency metrics such as occupancy rates, average length of stay, time to service, patient satisfaction, physician performance, patient readmission rate, inpatient mortality rate, and <a href="/wiki/Case_mix_index" title="Case mix index">case mix index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the number of hospitalizations in the United States continued to grow and reached its peak in 1981 with 171 admissions per 1,000 Americans and 6,933 hospitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYThospital_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYThospital-48">[47]</a></sup> This trend subsequently reversed, with the rate of hospitalization falling by more than 10% and the number of US hospitals shrinking from 6,933 in 1981 to 5,534 in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aha2016_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-aha2016-50">[49]</a></sup> Occupancy rates also dropped from 77% in 1980 to 60% in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Among the reasons for this are the increasing availability of more complex care elsewhere such as at home or at the physicians' offices and also the less therapeutic and more life-threatening image of the hospitals in the eyes of the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYThospital_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYThospital-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCinfection_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDCinfection-52">[51]</a></sup> In the US, a patient may sleep in a hospital bed, but be considered outpatient and "under observation" if not formally admitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[52]</a></sup> In the US, inpatient stays are covered under Medicare Part A, but a hospital might keep a patient under observation which is only covered under Medicare Part B, and subjects the patient to additional coinsurance costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[52]</a></sup> In 2013, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centers_for_Medicare_and_Medicaid_Services" title="Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services">Center for Medicare and Medicaid Services</a> (CMS) introduced a "two-midnight" rule for inpatient admissions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-54">[53]</a></sup> intended to reduce an increasing number of long-term "observation" stays being used for reimbursement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[52]</a></sup> This rule was later dropped in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-54">[53]</a></sup> In 2016 and 2017, healthcare reform and a continued decline in admissions resulted in US hospital-based healthcare systems performing poorly financially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> Microhospitals, with bed capacities of between eight and fifty, are expanding in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> Similarly, freestanding <a href="/wiki/Emergency_department" title="Emergency department">emergency rooms</a>, which transfer patients that require inpatient care to hospitals, were popularised in the 1970s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-57">[56]</a></sup> and have since expanded rapidly across the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">Funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hospital&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KB_Dubrava_Zagreb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1474" data-file-width="3643" decoding="async" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/KB_Dubrava_Zagreb.jpg/220px-KB_Dubrava_Zagreb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/KB_Dubrava_Zagreb.jpg/330px-KB_Dubrava_Zagreb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/KB_Dubrava_Zagreb.jpg/440px-KB_Dubrava_Zagreb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KB_Dubrava_Zagreb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Clinical_Hospital_Dubrava" title="Clinical Hospital Dubrava">Clinical Hospital Dubrava</a> in Zagreb, Croatia</div></div></div>
<p>Modern hospitals derive funding from a variety of sources. They may be funded by private payment and <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a> or <a href="/wiki/Public_expenditure" title="Public expenditure">public expenditure</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charitable_donation" title="Charitable donation">charitable donations</a>.
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> delivers health care to legal residents funded by the state "free at the point of delivery", and emergency care free to anyone regardless of nationality or status. Due to the need for hospitals to prioritise their limited resources, there is a tendency in countries with such systems for 'waiting lists' for non-crucial treatment, so those who can afford it may take out private health care to access treatment more quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GROW_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-GROW-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, hospitals typically operate privately and in some cases on a <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">for-profit</a> basis, such as <a href="/wiki/HCA_Healthcare" title="HCA Healthcare">HCA Healthcare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USATODAY_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-USATODAY-59">[58]</a></sup> The list of procedures and their prices are billed with a <a href="/wiki/Chargemaster" title="Chargemaster">chargemaster</a>; however, these prices may be lower for health care obtained within <a href="/wiki/Hospital_network" title="Hospital network">healthcare networks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> Legislation requires hospitals to provide care to patients in life-threatening emergency situations regardless of the patient's ability to pay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Privately funded hospitals which admit uninsured patients in emergency situations incur direct financial losses, such as in the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USATODAY_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-USATODAY-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quality_and_safety">Quality and safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hospital&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Quality and safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As the quality of health care has increasingly become an issue around the world, hospitals have increasingly had to pay serious attention to this matter. Independent external assessment of quality is one of the most powerful ways to assess this aspect of health care, and <a href="/wiki/Hospital_accreditation" title="Hospital accreditation">hospital accreditation</a> is one means by which this is achieved. In many parts of the world such accreditation is sourced from other countries, a phenomenon known as <a href="/wiki/International_healthcare_accreditation" title="International healthcare accreditation">international healthcare accreditation</a>, by groups such as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Accreditation_Canada&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Accreditation Canada (page does not exist)">Accreditation Canada</a> from Canada, the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Commission" title="Joint Commission">Joint Commission</a> from the US, the <a href="/wiki/Trent_Accreditation_Scheme" title="Trent Accreditation Scheme">Trent Accreditation Scheme</a> from Great Britain, and the <i><a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haute_Autorit%C3%A9_de_sant%C3%A9" title="fr:Haute Autorité de santé">Haute Autorité de santé</a></i> (HAS) from France.  In England hospitals are monitored by the <a href="/wiki/Care_Quality_Commission" title="Care Quality Commission">Care Quality Commission</a>. In 2020 they turned their attention to <a href="/wiki/Hospital_food" title="Hospital food">hospital food</a> standards after  seven patient deaths from listeria linked to pre-packaged sandwiches and salads in 2019, saying "Nutrition and hydration is part of a patient’s recovery."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> noted in 2011 that going into hospital was far riskier than flying.  Globally the chance of a patient being subject to an error was about 10%  and the chance of death resulting from an error was about 1 in 300 according to <a href="/wiki/Liam_Donaldson" title="Liam Donaldson">Liam Donaldson</a>. 7% of hospitalised patients in developed countries, and 10% in developing countries, acquire at least one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Health_Care-Associated_Infection" title="Health Care-Associated Infection">health care-associated infection</a>. In the USA 1.7 million infections are acquired in hospital each year, leading to 100,000 deaths, figures much worse than in Europe where there were 4.5 million infections and 37,000 deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hospital&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UVa_Medical_Center.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="402" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/UVa_Medical_Center.jpg/220px-UVa_Medical_Center.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/UVa_Medical_Center.jpg/330px-UVa_Medical_Center.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/UVa_Medical_Center.jpg/440px-UVa_Medical_Center.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UVa_Medical_Center.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The medical center at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a> shows the growing trend for modern architecture in hospitals.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NHS_NNUH_entrance.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/NHS_NNUH_entrance.jpg/180px-NHS_NNUH_entrance.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/NHS_NNUH_entrance.jpg/270px-NHS_NNUH_entrance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/NHS_NNUH_entrance.jpg/360px-NHS_NNUH_entrance.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NHS_NNUH_entrance.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_and_Norwich_University_Hospital" title="Norfolk and Norwich University Hospital">Norfolk and Norwich University Hospital</a> in the UK, showing the utilitarian architecture of many modern hospitals</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fawcett_Memorial_Hospital_Chapel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fawcett_Memorial_Hospital_Chapel.jpg/180px-Fawcett_Memorial_Hospital_Chapel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fawcett_Memorial_Hospital_Chapel.jpg/270px-Fawcett_Memorial_Hospital_Chapel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fawcett_Memorial_Hospital_Chapel.jpg/360px-Fawcett_Memorial_Hospital_Chapel.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fawcett_Memorial_Hospital_Chapel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hospital chapel at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fawcett_Memorial_Hospital" title="Fawcett Memorial Hospital">Fawcett Memorial Hospital</a> (<a href="/wiki/Port_Charlotte,_Florida" title="Port Charlotte, Florida">Port Charlotte, Florida</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Modern hospital buildings are designed to minimise the effort of medical personnel and the possibility of contamination while maximising the efficiency of the whole system. Travel time for personnel within the hospital and the transportation of patients between units is facilitated and minimised. The building also should be built to accommodate heavy departments such as radiology and operating rooms while space for special wiring, plumbing, and waste disposal must be allowed for in the design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, many hospitals, even those considered "modern", are the product of continual and often badly managed growth over decades or even centuries, with utilitarian new sections added on as needs and finances dictate. As a result, Dutch architectural historian Cor Wagenaar has called many hospitals:
</p>
<dl><dd><i>"... built catastrophes, anonymous institutional complexes run by vast bureaucracies, and totally unfit for the purpose they have been designed for ... They are hardly ever functional, and instead of making patients feel at home, they produce stress and anxiety."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODE_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODE-65">[64]</a></sup></i></dd></dl>
<p>Some newer hospitals now try to re-establish design that takes the patient's psychological needs into account, such as providing more fresh air, better views and more pleasant colour schemes. These ideas harken back to the late eighteenth century, when the concept of providing fresh air and access to the 'healing powers of nature' were first employed by hospital architects in improving their buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODE_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-ODE-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The research of <a href="/wiki/British_Medical_Association" title="British Medical Association">British Medical Association</a> is showing that good hospital design can reduce patient's recovery time. Exposure to daylight is effective in reducing depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> Single-sex accommodation help ensure that patients are treated in privacy and with dignity. Exposure to nature and hospital gardens is also important – looking out windows improves patients' moods and reduces blood pressure and stress level. Open windows in patient rooms have also demonstrated some evidence of beneficial outcomes by improving airflow and increased microbial diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> Eliminating long corridors can reduce nurses' fatigue and stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another ongoing major development is the change from a ward-based system (where patients are accommodated in communal rooms, separated by movable partitions) to one in which they are accommodated in individual rooms. The ward-based system has been described as very efficient, especially for the medical staff, but is considered to be more stressful for patients and detrimental to their privacy. A major constraint on providing all patients with their own rooms is however found in the higher cost of building and operating such a hospital; this causes some hospitals to charge for private rooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFHall2008">Hall, Daniel (December 2008). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2605310" rel="nofollow">"Altar and Table: A phenomenology of the surgeon-priest"</a>. <i>Yale Journal of Biology and Medicine</i>. <b>81</b> (4): 193–98. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2605310" rel="nofollow">2605310</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19099050" rel="nofollow">19099050</a>. <q>Although physicians were available in varying capacities in ancient Rome and Athens, the institution of a hospital dedicated to the care of the sick was a distinctly Christian innovation rooted in the monastic virtue and practise of hospitality. Arranged around the monastery were concentric rings of buildings in which the life and work of the monastic community was ordered. The outer ring of buildings served as a hostel in which travellers were received and boarded. The inner ring served as a place where the monastic community could care for the sick, the poor and the infirm. Monks were frequently familiar with the medicine available at that time, growing medicinal plants on the monastery grounds and applying remedies as indicated. As such, many of the practicing physicians of the Middle Ages were also clergy.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Yale+Journal+of+Biology+and+Medicine&amp;rft.atitle=Altar+and+Table%3A+A+phenomenology+of+the+surgeon-priest&amp;rft.volume=81&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=193-98&amp;rft.date=2008-12&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC2605310%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F19099050&amp;rft.aulast=Hall&amp;rft.aufirst=Daniel&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC2605310&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHospital"></span></span>
